   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Zambia</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the African nation. For the 18th-century BC king of Isin, see <a href="/wiki/Zambiya" title="Zambiya">Zambiya</a>. For the part of East Prussia, see <a href="/wiki/Sambia_Peninsula" title="Sambia Peninsula">Sambia Peninsula</a>.
      </div> 
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country at the crossroads of Central, Southern and East Africa
      </div>

      <p><b>Zambia</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>-/</a></span></span>), which is officially the <b>Republic of Zambia</b> (<a href="/wiki/Tonga_language_(Zambia_and_Zimbabwe)" title="Tonga language (Zambia and Zimbabwe)">Tonga</a>: <i>Cisi ca Zambia</i>; <a href="/wiki/Lozi_language" title="Lozi language">Lozi</a>: <i>Naha la Zambia</i>; <a href="/wiki/Chewa_language" title="Chewa language">Nyanja</a>: <i>Dziko la Zambia</i>), is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> at the crossroads of <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern</a> and East Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Its neighbors are the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> to the north-east, <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> to the southeast, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> to the southwest, and <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> to the west. The capital city of Zambia is <a href="/wiki/Lusaka" title="Lusaka">Lusaka</a>, located in the south-central part of Zambia. The population is concentrated mainly around <a href="/wiki/Lusaka" title="Lusaka">Lusaka</a> in the south and the <a href="/wiki/Copperbelt_Province" title="Copperbelt Province">Copperbelt Province</a> to the north, the core economic hubs of the country. </p>
      <p>Originally inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> peoples, the region was affected by the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/13th_century" title="13th century">thirteenth century</a>. Following <a href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">European explorers</a> in the eighteenth century, the British <a href="/wiki/Colonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Colonisation">colonised</a> the region into the <a href="/wiki/British_protectorate" title="British protectorate">British protectorates</a> of <a href="/wiki/Barotziland-North-Western_Rhodesia" title="Barotziland-North-Western Rhodesia">Barotziland-North-Western Rhodesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North-Eastern_Rhodesia" title="North-Eastern Rhodesia">North-Eastern Rhodesia</a> towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">nineteenth century.</a> These were merged in 1911 to form <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a>. For most of the colonial period, Zambia was governed by an administration appointed from London with the advice of the <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 24 October 1964, Zambia became independent of the United Kingdom and prime minister <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kaunda" title="Kenneth Kaunda">Kenneth Kaunda</a> became the inaugural <a href="/wiki/President_of_Zambia" title="President of Zambia">president</a>. Kaunda's <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/United_National_Independence_Party" title="United National Independence Party">United National Independence Party</a> (UNIP) maintained power from 1964 until 1991. <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kaunda" title="Kenneth Kaunda">Kaunda</a> played a key role in regional diplomacy, cooperating closely with the United States in search of solutions to conflicts in Southern Rhodesia (Zimbabwe), Angola, and Namibia.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> From 1972 to 1991 Zambia was a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a> with the UNIP as the sole legal political party under the motto "One Zambia, One Nation". Kaunda was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Chiluba" title="Frederick Chiluba">Frederick Chiluba</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social-democratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Movement_for_Multi-Party_Democracy" title="Movement for Multi-Party Democracy">Movement for Multi-Party Democracy</a> in 1991, beginning a period of social-economic growth and government <a href="/wiki/Decentralisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Decentralisation">decentralisation</a>. Zambia has since become a multi-party state and has experienced several <a href="/wiki/Peaceful_transition_of_power" title="Peaceful transition of power">peaceful transitions of power</a>. </p>
      <p>Zambia contains abundant natural resources, including minerals, wildlife, forestry, freshwater and arable land.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> named Zambia one of the world's fastest economically reformed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa</a> (COMESA) is headquartered in Lusaka. </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_(name)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesia (name)">Rhodesia (name)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The territory of what is now Zambia was known as <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a> from 1911 to 1964, October. It was renamed Zambia at independence in 1964, October. The new name of Zambia was derived from the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> River (Zambezi may mean "grand river").<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Zambia" title="History of Zambia">History of Zambia</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_era">Prehistoric era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistoric era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Archaeological_excavation" title="Archaeological excavation">Archaeological excavation</a> work on the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Zambezi Valley">Zambezi Valley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalambo_Falls" title="Kalambo Falls">Kalambo Falls</a> show a succession of human cultures. Ancient camping site tools near the <a href="/wiki/Kalambo_Falls" title="Kalambo Falls">Kalambo Falls</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dated</a> to more than 36,000 years ago. </p>
      <p>The fossil skull remains of <a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">Broken Hill Man (also known as Kabwe Man)</a>, dated between 300,000 and 125,000 years BC, further shows that the area was inhabited by early humans.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">Broken Hill Man</a> was discovered in Zambia to be specific in Kabwe District. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Khoisan_and_Batwa">Khoisan and Batwa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Khoisan and Batwa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:NsaluCave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/NsaluCave.jpg/220px-NsaluCave.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/NsaluCave.jpg/330px-NsaluCave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/NsaluCave.jpg/440px-NsaluCave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:NsaluCave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Ancient (but graffitied) Rock Art in Nsalu Cave, Kasanka National Park in North-Central Zambia.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The area of modern Zambia is known to have been inhabited by the <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twa" title="Twa">Batwa</a> peoples until around AD 300, when migrating <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> began to settle around these areas.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> It is believed the <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> people groups originated in East Africa and spread southwards around 150,000 years ago. The Twa people were split into two groups. One, the <a href="/wiki/Kafwe_Twa" title="Kafwe Twa">Kafwe Twa</a>, lived around the <a href="/wiki/Kafue_Flats" title="Kafue Flats">Kafue Flats</a> while the other, the <a href="/wiki/Lukanga_Twa" title="Lukanga Twa">Lukanga Twa</a>, lived around the <a href="/wiki/Lukanga_Swamp" title="Lukanga Swamp">Lukanga Swamp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Many examples of ancient rock art in Zambia, like those of the <a href="/wiki/Mwela_Rock_Paintings" title="Mwela Rock Paintings">Mwela Rock Paintings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumbwa_Caves" title="Mumbwa Caves">Mumbwa Caves</a>, and Nachikufu Cave, are attributed to these early <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> groups. The Khoisan and especially the Twa formed a <a href="/wiki/Patron-client_relationship" class="mw-redirect" title="Patron-client relationship">patron-client relationship</a> with farming Bantu peoples across central and southern Africa but were eventually either displaced by or absorbed into the Bantu groups. </p> 
      <h3><span id="The_Bantu_.28Abantu.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Bantu_(Abantu)">The Bantu (Abantu)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The Bantu (Abantu)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The Bantu people or Abantu (meaning people) are an enormous and diverse <a href="/wiki/Ethnolinguistic_group" title="Ethnolinguistic group">ethnolinguistic group</a> that comprise the majority of people in much of <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern</a> and Central Africa. Due to Zambia's location at the crossroads of Central Africa, Southern Africa, and the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a>, the history of the people that constitute modern Zambians is a history of these three regions. </p>
      <p>Many of the historical events in these three regions happened simultaneously, and thus Zambia's history, like many African nation's, cannot be presented perfectly chronologically. The early history of the peoples of modern Zambia is deduced from oral records, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, and written records, mostly from non-Africans.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bantu_origins">Bantu origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Bantu origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Batonga_women.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Batonga_women.png/220px-Batonga_women.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Batonga_women.png/330px-Batonga_women.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Batonga_women.png/440px-Batonga_women.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="350"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Batonga_women.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Batonga" class="mw-redirect" title="Batonga">Batonga</a> fisherwomen in Southern Zambia. Women have and continue to play important roles in many African societies.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Bantu people originally lived in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West</a> and Central Africa around what is today <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>. Around 4000 to 3000 years ago they began a millennia-long expansion into much of the continent. This event has been called the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a>; it was one of the largest human migrations in history. The Bantu are believed to have been the first to have brought <a href="/wiki/Ferrous_metallurgy" title="Ferrous metallurgy">iron working</a> technology into large parts of Africa. The Bantu Expansion happened primarily through two routes: a western one via the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin">Congo Basin</a> and an eastern one via the African Great Lakes.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Bantu_settlement">First Bantu settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: First Bantu settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The first Bantu people to arrive in Zambia came through the eastern route via the African Great Lakes. They arrived around the first millennium C.E, and among them were the <a href="/wiki/Tonga_people_of_Zambia_and_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonga people of Zambia and Zimbabwe">Tonga people</a> (also called Ba-Tonga, "Ba-" meaning "men") and the <a href="/wiki/Ila_people" title="Ila people">Ba-Ila</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mwanga_people" title="Mwanga people">Namwanga</a> and other related groups who settled around <a href="/wiki/Southern_Province,_Zambia" title="Southern Province, Zambia">Southern Zambia</a> near <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>. Ba-Tonga oral records indicate that they came from the east near the "big sea". </p>
      <p>They were later joined by the <a href="/wiki/Tumbuka_people" title="Tumbuka people">Ba-Tumbuka</a> who settled around <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Zambia" title="Eastern Province, Zambia">Eastern Zambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>. </p>
      <p>These first Bantu people lived in large villages. They lacked an organised unit under a chief or headman and worked as a community and help each other in times of field preparation for their crops. Villages moved around frequently as the soil became exhausted as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> technique of planting crops. The people also keep large herds of cattle, which formed an important part of their societies.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg/220px-Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg/330px-Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg/440px-Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="900"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Great-Zimbabwe-ruins-outer-walls-3-1200.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Ruins of <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kalanga_people" title="Kalanga people">Kalanga</a>/<a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a> rulers of this kingdom dominated trade at Ingombe Ilede.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first Bantu communities in Zambia were highly self-sufficient. Early <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">European missionaries</a> who settled in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Province,_Zambia" title="Southern Province, Zambia">Southern Zambia</a> noted the independence of these Bantu societies. One of these missionaries noted: "[If] weapons for war, hunting, and domestic purposes are needed, the [Tonga] man goes to the hills and digs until he finds the iron ore. He smelts it and with the iron thus obtained makes axes, hoes, and other useful implements. He burns wood and makes charcoal for his forge. His bellows are made from the skins of animals and the pipes are clay tile, and the anvil and hammers are also pieces of the iron he has obtained. He moulds, welds, shapes, and performs all the work of the ordinary blacksmith."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>These early Bantu settlers also participated in the trade at the site <a href="/wiki/Ingombe_Ilede" title="Ingombe Ilede">Ingombe Ilede</a> (which translate sleeping cow in <a href="/wiki/Chitonga" class="mw-redirect" title="Chitonga">Chi-Tonga</a> because the fallen baobab tree appears to resembles a cow) in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Province,_Zambia" title="Southern Province, Zambia">Southern Zambia</a>. At this trading site they met numerous <a href="/wiki/Kalanga_people" title="Kalanga people">Kalanga</a>/<a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a> traders from <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> traders from the East African <a href="/wiki/Swahili_coast" title="Swahili coast">Swahili coast</a>. Ingombe Ilede was one of the most important trading posts for rulers of Great Zimbabwe, others being the Swahili port cities like <a href="/wiki/Sofala" title="Sofala">Sofala</a>. </p>
      <p>The goods traded at Ingombe Ilede included fabrics, beads, gold, and bangles. Some of these items came from what is today southern <a href="/wiki/The_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="The Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa Kisiwani</a> while others came from as far away as <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, China and the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The African traders were later joined by the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The decline of Great Zimbabwe, due to increasing trade competition from other <a href="/wiki/Kalanga_people" title="Kalanga people">Kalanga</a>/<a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a> kingdoms like <a href="/wiki/Khami" title="Khami">Khami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Mutapa</a>, spelt the end of <a href="/wiki/Ingombe_Ilede" title="Ingombe Ilede">Ingombe Ilede</a>. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Bantu_settlement">Second Bantu settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Second Bantu settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The second mass settlement of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu people</a> into Zambia was of people groups that are believed to have taken the western route of the Bantu migration through the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin">Congo Basin</a>. These Bantu people spent the majority of their existence in what is today the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a> and are ancestors of the majority of modern Zambians.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>While there is some evidence that the <a href="/wiki/Bemba_people" title="Bemba people">Bemba people</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bemba_people" title="Bemba people">AbaBemba</a> have a strong ancient connection to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kongo" title="Kingdom of Kongo">Kongo Kingdom</a> through <a href="/wiki/Kongo_people" title="Kongo people">BaKongo</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Manikongo" title="Manikongo">Mwene Kongo VIII Mvemba</a>, this is not well documented. </p>
      <p><b>Luba-Lunda states</b> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mwata.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mwata.jpg/220px-Mwata.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mwata.jpg/330px-Mwata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mwata.jpg/440px-Mwata.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="523"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mwata.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Drawing of the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Lunda_people" title="Lunda people">Lunda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazembe" title="Kazembe">Mwata Kazembe</a>, receiving Portuguese in the royal courtyard in the 1800s
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Bemba, along with other related groups like the <a href="/wiki/Lamba_people_(Zambia)" title="Lamba people (Zambia)">Lamba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lala-Bisa_language" title="Lala-Bisa language">Bisa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senga_people" title="Senga people">Senga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaonde_language" title="Kaonde language">Kaonde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swaka_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Swaka language">Swaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nkoya" class="mw-redirect" title="Nkoya">Nkoya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soli_language" title="Soli language">Soli</a>, formed integral parts of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Luba" title="Kingdom of Luba">Luba Kingdom</a> in Upemba part of the Democratic Republic of Congo and have a strong relation to the <a href="/wiki/Luba_people" title="Luba people">BaLuba people</a>. The area which the Luba Kingdom occupied has been inhabited by early farmers and iron workers since the 300s C.E. </p>
      <p>Over time these communities learned to use <a href="/wiki/Fishing_net" title="Fishing net">nets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harpoon" title="Harpoon">harpoons</a>, make <a href="/wiki/Dugout_canoe" title="Dugout canoe">dugout canoes</a>, clear <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a> through swamps and make dams as high as 2.5 meters. As a result, they grew a <a href="/wiki/Economic_diversity" title="Economic diversity">diverse economy</a> trading fish, copper and iron items and salt for goods from other parts of Africa, like the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_coast" title="Swahili coast">Swahili coast</a> and, later on, the Portuguese. From these communities arose the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Luba" title="Kingdom of Luba">Luba Kingdom</a> in the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-metmuseum.org_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metmuseum.org-26">[26]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Luba Kingdom was a large kingdom with a <a href="/wiki/Centralized_government" title="Centralized government">centralised government</a> and smaller independent <a href="/wiki/Chiefdom" title="Chiefdom">chiefdoms</a>. It had large trading networks that linked the forests in the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin">Congo Basin</a> and the mineral-rich plateaus of what is today <a href="/wiki/Copperbelt_Province" title="Copperbelt Province">Copperbelt Province</a> and stretched from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic coast</a> to the Indian Ocean coast. The arts were also held in high esteem in the kingdom, and artisans were held in high regard.<sup id="cite_ref-metmuseum.org_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metmuseum.org-26">[26]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Literature was well developed in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Luba" title="Kingdom of Luba">Luba Kingdom</a>. One renowned <a href="/wiki/Luba_people" title="Luba people">Luba</a> genesis story that articulated the distinction between two types of Luba emperors goes as follows: </p>
      <p>"<a href="/wiki/Kongolo_Mwamba" title="Kongolo Mwamba">Nkongolo Mwamba</a>, the red king, and <a href="/wiki/Ilunga_Mbili" title="Ilunga Mbili">Ilunga Mbidi Kiluwe</a>, a prince of legendary black complexion. Nkongolo Mwamba is the drunken and cruel despot, Ilunga Mbidi Kiluwe the refined and gentle prince. Nkongolo the Red is a man without manners, a man who eats in public, gets drunk, and cannot control himself, whereas [Ilunga] Mbidi Kiluwe is a man of reservation, obsessed with good manners; he does not eat in public, controls his language and his behaviour, and keeps a distance from the vices and modus vivendi of ordinary people. Nkongolo Mwamba symbolizes the embodiment of tyranny, whereas Mbidi Kiluwe remains the admired caring and compassionate kin."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lunda_houses-1854.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Lunda_houses-1854.jpg/170px-Lunda_houses-1854.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Lunda_houses-1854.jpg/255px-Lunda_houses-1854.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Lunda_houses-1854.jpg/340px-Lunda_houses-1854.jpg 2x" data-file-width="537" data-file-height="760"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lunda_houses-1854.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A drawing of <a href="/wiki/Lunda_people" title="Lunda people">Lunda</a> houses by a Portuguese visitor. The size of the doorways relative to the building emphasizes the scale of the buildings.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the same region of Southern Congo the <a href="/wiki/Lunda_people" title="Lunda people">Lunda people</a> were made into a satellite of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Luba" title="Kingdom of Luba">Luba empire</a> and adopted forms of Luba culture and governance, thus becoming the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lunda" title="Kingdom of Lunda">Lunda Empire</a> to the south. According to Lunda genesis myths, a Luba hunter named <a href="/wiki/Tshibinda_Ilunga" title="Tshibinda Ilunga">Chibinda Ilunga</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Ilunga_Mbili" title="Ilunga Mbili">Ilunga Mbidi Kiluwe</a>, introduced the Luba model of statecraft to the Lunda sometime around 1600 when he married a local Lunda princess named Lueji and was granted control of her kingdom. Most rulers who claimed descent from Luba ancestors were integrated into the Luba empire. The Lunda kings, however, remained separate and actively expanded their political and economic dominance over the region.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-28">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Lunda, like its parent state Luba, also traded with both coasts, the Atlantic and Indian Oceans. While ruler <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_the_Lunda_Empire" title="List of rulers of the Lunda Empire">Mwaant Yaav Naweej</a> had established trade routes to the Atlantic coast and initiated direct contact with European traders eager for <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">slaves</a> and forest products and controlling the regional Copper trade, and settlements around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Mweru" title="Lake Mweru">Lake Mweru</a> regulated commerce with the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_coast" title="Swahili coast">East African coast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-28">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Luba-Lunda states eventually declined as a result of both <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> in the west and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_slave_trade" title="Indian Ocean slave trade">Indian Ocean slave trade</a> in the east and wars with breakaway factions of the kingdoms. The <a href="/wiki/Chokwe_people" title="Chokwe people">Chokwe</a>, a group that is closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Luvale_people" title="Luvale people">Luvale</a> and formed a Lunda satellite state, initially suffered from the European demand for slaves, but once they broke away from the Lunda state, they themselves became notorious slave traders, exporting slaves to both coasts. </p>
      <p>The Chokwe eventually were defeated by the other ethnic groups and the Portuguese.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> This instability caused the collapse of the Luba-Lunda states and a dispersal of people into various parts of Zambia from the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>. The majority of Zambians trace their ancestry to the Luba-Lunda and surrounding Central African states.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><b>The Maravi Confederacy</b> </p>
      <p> In the 1200s, before the founding of the Luba-Lunda states, a group of Bantu people started migrating from the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin">Congo Basin</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Mweru" title="Lake Mweru">Lake Mweru</a> then finally settled around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Malawi" title="Lake Malawi">Lake Malawi</a>. These migrants are believed to have been one of the inhabitants around the <a href="/wiki/Upemba_Depression" title="Upemba Depression">Upemba</a> area in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>. By the 1400s these groups of migrants collectively called the Maravi, and most prominently among them was the <a href="/wiki/Chewa_people" title="Chewa people">Chewa people</a> (AChewa), who started assimilating other Bantu groups like the <a href="/wiki/Tumbuka_people" title="Tumbuka people">Tumbuka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Team_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Team-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Kalonga.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Kalonga.jpg/220px-Kalonga.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Kalonga.jpg/330px-Kalonga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Kalonga.jpg/440px-Kalonga.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kalonga.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The kalonga (ruler) of the <a href="/wiki/Chewa_people" title="Chewa people">AChewa</a> today descends from the kalonga of the Maravi Empire.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1480 the <a href="/wiki/Maravi" title="Maravi">Maravi</a> Empire was founded by the kalonga (paramount chief of the Maravi) from the Phiri clan, one of the main clans, with the others being Banda, Mwale and Nkhoma. The Maravi Empire stretched from the Indian Ocean through what today is <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> to Zambia and large parts of <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>. The political organization of the Maravi resembled that of the Luba and is believed to have originated from there. The primary export of the Maravi was ivory, which was transported to Swahili brokers.<sup id="cite_ref-Team_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Team-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iron was also manufactured and exported. In the 1590s the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> endeavoured to take monopoly over Maravi export trade. This attempt was met with outrage by the Maravi of Lundu, who unleashed their WaZimba armed force. The WaZimba sacked the Portuguese trade towns of Tete, Sena and various other towns.<sup id="cite_ref-thinkafrica.net_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thinkafrica.net-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Maravi are also believed to have brought the traditions that would become <a href="/wiki/Nyau" title="Nyau">Nyau</a> secret society from <a href="/wiki/Upemba_Depression" title="Upemba Depression">Upemba</a>. The Nyau form the cosmology or indigenous religion of the people of Maravi. The <a href="/wiki/Nyau" title="Nyau">Nyau</a> society consists of ritual dance performances and masks used for the dances; this belief system spread around the region.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Maravi declined as a result of succession disputes within the confederacy, attack by the <a href="/wiki/Ngoni_people" title="Ngoni people">Ngoni</a> and slave raids from the <a href="/wiki/Yao_people_(East_Africa)" title="Yao people (East Africa)">Yao</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thinkafrica.net_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thinkafrica.net-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><b>Mutapa Empire and Mfecane</b> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg/220px-Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg/330px-Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg/440px-Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="641" data-file-height="473"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ngoni_Chiefs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Three young <a href="/wiki/Ngoni_people" title="Ngoni people">Ngoni</a> chiefs. The Ngoni made their way into <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Zambia" title="Eastern Province, Zambia">Eastern Zambia</a> from <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu</a> in South Africa. They eventually assimilated into the local ethic groups.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a> was in decline, one of its prince's, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nyatsimba_Mutota&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nyatsimba Mutota (page does not exist)">Nyatsimba Mutota</a>, broke away from the state forming a new empire called <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Mutapa</a>. The title of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Mwene Mutapa</a>, meaning "Ravager of the Lands", was bestowed on him and subsequent rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Mutapa Empire</a> ruled territory between the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limpopo" title="Limpopo">Limpopo</a> rivers, in what is now Zambia, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, from the 14th to the 17th century. By its, peak Mutapa had conquered the Dande area of the <a href="/wiki/Tonga_people_(Zambia_and_Zimbabwe)" title="Tonga people (Zambia and Zimbabwe)">Tonga</a> and Tavara. The Mutapa Empire predominately engaged in the Indian Ocean transcontinental trade with and via the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">WaSwahili</a>. The primary exported gold and ivory for silk and ceramics from Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Like their contemporaries in <a href="/wiki/Maravi" title="Maravi">Maravi</a>, Mutapa had problems with the arriving <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> traders. The peak of this uneasy relationship was reached when the Portuguese attempted to influence the kingdoms internal affairs by establishing markets in the kingdom and converting the population to Christianity. This action caused outrage by the <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> WaSwahili living in the capital, this chaos gave the Portuguese the excuse they were searching for to warrant an attack on the kingdom and try to control its gold mines and ivory routes. This attack failed when the Portuguese succumb to disease along the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> river.<sup id="cite_ref-Mutapa_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mutapa-36">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 1600s internal disputes and civil war began the decline of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Mutapa</a>. The weakened kingdom was finally conquered by the Portuguese and was eventually taken over by rival <a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a> states.<sup id="cite_ref-Mutapa_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mutapa-36">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p> The <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> also had vast estates, known as Prazos, and they used slaves and ex-slaves as security guards and hunters. They trained the men in military tactics and gave them guns. These men became expert elephant hunters and were known as the <a href="/wiki/Chikunda" title="Chikunda">Chikunda</a>. After the decline of the Portuguese the <a href="/wiki/Chikunda" title="Chikunda">Chikunda</a> made their way to Zambia.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Litunga.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Litunga.jpg/220px-Litunga.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Litunga.jpg/330px-Litunga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Litunga.jpg/440px-Litunga.jpg 2x" data-file-width="730" data-file-height="524"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Litunga.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Inside the palace of the <a href="/wiki/Litunga" title="Litunga">Litunga</a>, ruler of the Lozi. Due to the flooding on the Zambezi, the Litunga has two palaces one of which is on higher ground. The movement of Litunga to higher land is celebrated at the <a href="/wiki/Kuomboka" title="Kuomboka">Kuomboka</a> Ceremony
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>It is hypothesised by <a href="/wiki/Julian_Cobbing" title="Julian Cobbing">Julian Cobbing</a> that the presence of early <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">Europeans</a> <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">slave trading</a> and attempts to control resources in various parts of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu Speaking Africa</a> caused the gradual <a href="/wiki/Militarization" title="Militarization">militarization</a> of the people in the region. This can be observed with the Maravi's WaZimba warrior cast who once defeating the Portuguese remained quite militaristic afterwards. </p>
      <p>The Portuguese presence in the region was also a major reason for the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Rozvi_Empire" title="Rozvi Empire">Rozvi Empire</a>, a breakaway state of Mutapa. The ruler of the Rozvi, <a href="/wiki/Changamire_Dombo" class="mw-redirect" title="Changamire Dombo">Changamire Dombo</a>, became one of the most powerful leaders in South-Central Africa's history. Under his leadership, the Rozvi defeated the Portuguese and expelled them from their trading posts along the Zambezi river.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>But perhaps the most notable instance of this increased militarization was the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Shaka" title="Shaka">Shaka</a>. Pressures from the <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English colonialists</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape</a> and increased militarization of the Zulu resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Mfecane" title="Mfecane">Mfecane</a> (the crushing). The Zulu expanded by assimilating the women and children of tribes they defeated, if the men of these <a href="/wiki/Nguni_people" title="Nguni people">Nguni tribes</a> escaped slaughter, they used the military tactics of the Zulu to attack other groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Mfecane_{{!}}_African_history_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mfecane_{{!}}_African_history-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>This caused mass displacements, wars and raids throughout Southern, Central and Eastern Africa as <a href="/wiki/Nguni_people" title="Nguni people">Nguni</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ngoni_people" title="Ngoni people">Ngoni</a> tribes made their way throughout the region and is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Mfecane" title="Mfecane">Mfecane</a>. The arriving Nguni under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Zwangendaba" title="Zwangendaba">Zwagendaba</a> crossed the Zambezi river moving northwards. The Ngoni were the final blow to the already weakened <a href="/wiki/Maravi" title="Maravi">Maravi</a> Empire. Many Nguni eventually settled around what is today Zambia, <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> and assimilated into neighboring tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-Mfecane_{{!}}_African_history_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mfecane_{{!}}_African_history-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Western_Province,_Zambia" title="Western Province, Zambia">western part</a> of Zambia, another Southern African group of <a href="/wiki/Sotho-Tswana_peoples" title="Sotho-Tswana peoples">Sotho-Tswana</a> heritage called the <a href="/wiki/Kololo_people" title="Kololo people">Kololo</a> manage to conquer the local inhabitants who were migrants from the fallen <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Luba" title="Kingdom of Luba">Luba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lunda" title="Kingdom of Lunda">Lunda states</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Luyana_language" title="Luyana language">Luyana</a> or Aluyi. The Luyana established the <a href="/wiki/Barotseland" title="Barotseland">Barotse Kingdom</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Barotse_Floodplain" title="Barotse Floodplain">floodplains of the Zambezi</a> upon their arrival from Katanga. Under the Kololo, the Kololo language was imposed upon the Luyana until the Luyana revolted and overthrew the Kololo by this time the Luyana language was largely forgotten and a new hybrid language emerged, <a href="/wiki/Lozi_language" title="Lozi language">SiLozi</a> and the Luyana began to refer to themselves as <a href="/wiki/Lozi_people" title="Lozi people">Lozi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At the end of the 18th century, some of the <a href="/wiki/Mbunda_people" title="Mbunda people">Mbunda</a> migrated to <a href="/wiki/Barotseland" title="Barotseland">Barotseland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongu" title="Mongu">Mongu</a> upon the migration of among others, the <a href="/wiki/Rulers_of_Mbundaland" title="Rulers of Mbundaland">Ciyengele</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The Aluyi and their leader, the Litunga Mulambwa, especially valued the Mbunda for their fighting ability. </p>
      <p>By the late 19th century, most of the various peoples of Zambia were established in their current areas. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_Period">Colonial Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Colonial Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europeans">Europeans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Europeans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:David_Livingstone_-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/David_Livingstone_-1.jpg/170px-David_Livingstone_-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/David_Livingstone_-1.jpg/255px-David_Livingstone_-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/David_Livingstone_-1.jpg/340px-David_Livingstone_-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="842" data-file-height="1024"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:David_Livingstone_-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An 1864 portrait of Scottish explorer and missionary <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>One of the earliest recorded European to visit the area was the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Lacerda" title="Francisco de Lacerda">Francisco de Lacerda</a> in the late 18th century. Lacerda led an expedition from Mozambique to the Kazembe region in Zambia (with the goal of exploring and to crossing Southern Africa from coast to coast for the first time),<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and died during the expedition in 1798. The expedition was from then on led by his friend Francisco Pinto.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This territory, located between <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Portuguese Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Angola" title="Portuguese Angola">Portuguese Angola</a>, was claimed and explored by Portugal in that period. </p>
      <p>Other European visitors followed in the 19th century. The most prominent of these was <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a>, who had a vision of ending the <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">slave trade</a> through the "3 Cs": Christianity, Commerce, and Civilisation. He was the first European to see the magnificent waterfalls on the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Zambezi River">Zambezi River</a> in 1855, naming them the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Falls" title="Victoria Falls">Victoria Falls</a> after <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> of the United Kingdom. He described them thus: "Scenes so lovely must have been gazed upon by angels in their flight".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Locally the falls are known as <a href="/wiki/Mosi-oa-Tunya" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosi-oa-Tunya">"Mosi-o-Tunya"</a> or "thundering smoke" in the Lozi or Kololo dialect. The town of <a href="/wiki/Livingstone,_Zambia" title="Livingstone, Zambia">Livingstone</a>, near the Falls, is named after him. Highly publicised accounts of his journeys motivated a wave of European visitors, missionaries and traders after his death in 1873.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><b>British South Africa Company</b> </p>
      <p>In 1888, the <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a> (BSA Company), led by <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>, obtained mineral rights from the <a href="/wiki/Litunga" title="Litunga">Litunga</a> of the Lozi people, the Paramount Chief of the <a href="/wiki/Lozi_people" title="Lozi people">Lozi (Ba-rotse)</a> for the area which later became <a href="/wiki/Barotziland-North-Western_Rhodesia" title="Barotziland-North-Western Rhodesia">Barotziland-North-Western Rhodesia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-livingstone_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livingstone-47">[47]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:CecilRhodes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/CecilRhodes.jpg/170px-CecilRhodes.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/CecilRhodes.jpg/255px-CecilRhodes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/CecilRhodes.jpg/340px-CecilRhodes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="564" data-file-height="752"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:CecilRhodes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Cecil Rhodes
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>To the east, in December 1897 a group of the <a href="/wiki/Ngoni_people" title="Ngoni people">Angoni or Ngoni</a> (originally from Zululand) rebelled under Tsinco, son of King <a href="/wiki/Mpezeni" title="Mpezeni">Mpezeni</a>, but the rebellion was put down,<sup id="cite_ref-unam_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unam-48">[48]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mpezeni" title="Mpezeni">Mpezeni</a> accepted the <a href="/wiki/Pax_Britannica" title="Pax Britannica">Pax Britannica</a>. That part of the country then came to be known as <a href="/wiki/North-Eastern_Rhodesia" title="North-Eastern Rhodesia">North-Eastern Rhodesia</a>. In 1895, Rhodes asked his American scout <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Russell_Burnham" title="Frederick Russell Burnham">Frederick Russell Burnham</a> to look for minerals and ways to improve river navigation in the region, and it was during this trek that Burnham discovered major copper deposits along the <a href="/wiki/Kafue_River" title="Kafue River">Kafue River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-burnham1899_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burnham1899-49">[49]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/North-Eastern_Rhodesia" title="North-Eastern Rhodesia">North-Eastern Rhodesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barotziland-North-Western_Rhodesia" title="Barotziland-North-Western Rhodesia">Barotziland-North-Western Rhodesia</a> were administered as separate units until 1911 when they were merged to form <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a>, a British protectorate. In 1923, the BSA Company ceded control of Northern Rhodesia to the British Government after the government decided not to renew the company's charter. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_colonisation">British colonisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: British colonisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In 1923, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>), a conquered territory which was also administered by the BSA Company, became a self-governing British colony. In 1924, after negotiations, the administration of Northern Rhodesia transferred to the British <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">Colonial Office</a>. </p>
      <p><b>Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland</b> In 1953, the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" title="Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland</a> grouped together Northern Rhodesia, Southern Rhodesia, and <a href="/wiki/Nyasaland_Protectorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nyasaland Protectorate">Nyasaland</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>) as a single semi-autonomous region. This was undertaken despite opposition from a sizeable minority of the population, who demonstrated against it in 1960–61.<sup id="cite_ref-infoplease_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infoplease-50">[50]</a></sup> Northern Rhodesia was the center of much of the turmoil and crisis characterizing the federation in its last years. Initially, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Nkumbula" title="Harry Nkumbula">Harry Nkumbula</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Zambian_African_National_Congress" title="Zambian African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC) led the campaign, which <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kaunda" title="Kenneth Kaunda">Kenneth Kaunda</a>'s United National Independence Party (UNIP) subsequently took up. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zambia123f.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Zambia123f.jpg/220px-Zambia123f.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Zambia123f.jpg/330px-Zambia123f.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Zambia123f.jpg/440px-Zambia123f.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1331" data-file-height="915"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zambia123f.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_David_Kaunda" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenneth David Kaunda">Kenneth Kaunda</a>, first Republican president, on a state visit to <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Romania" title="Socialist Republic of Romania">Romania</a> in 1970
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A two-stage election held in October and December 1962 resulted in an African majority in the legislative council and an uneasy coalition between the two African nationalist parties. The council passed resolutions calling for Northern Rhodesia's secession from the federation and demanding full internal self-government under a new constitution and a new <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Zambia" title="National Assembly of Zambia">National Assembly</a> based on a broader, more democratic franchise.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The federation was dissolved on 31 December 1963, and in January 1964, Kaunda won the only election for Prime Minister of Northern Rhodesia. The <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Northern_Rhodesia" title="Governor of Northern Rhodesia">Colonial Governor</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Dennison_Hone" title="Evelyn Dennison Hone">Evelyn Hone</a>, was very close to Kaunda and urged him to stand for the post. Soon after, there was an uprising in the north of the country known as the <a href="/wiki/Lumpa_Church" title="Lumpa Church">Lumpa Uprising</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Alice_Lenshina" title="Alice Lenshina">Alice Lenshina</a>&nbsp;– Kaunda's first internal conflict as leader of the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Northern Rhodesia became the Republic of Zambia on 24 October 1964, with <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kaunda" title="Kenneth Kaunda">Kenneth Kaunda</a> as the first president. At independence, despite its considerable mineral wealth, Zambia faced major challenges. Domestically, there were few trained and educated Zambians capable of running the government, and the economy was largely dependent on foreign expertise. This expertise was provided in part by <a href="/wiki/John_Willson_CMG" class="mw-redirect" title="John Willson CMG">John Willson CMG</a><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> There were over 70,000 Europeans resident in Zambia in 1964, and they remained of disproportionate economic significance.<sup id="cite_ref-Zambia_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zambia-54">[54]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_Independence">Post Independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Post Independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Kaunda's endorsement of <a href="/wiki/Patriotic_Front_(Zimbabwe)" title="Patriotic Front (Zimbabwe)">Patriotic Front</a> guerrillas conducting raids into neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">(Southern) Rhodesia</a> resulted in political tension and a militarisation of the border, leading to its closure in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-blackfire_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackfire-55">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kariba_Dam" title="Kariba Dam">Kariba hydroelectric</a> station on the Zambezi River provided sufficient capacity to satisfy the country's requirements for electricity, despite Rhodesian management. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:RhodesiaAllies1965.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RhodesiaAllies1965.png/220px-RhodesiaAllies1965.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RhodesiaAllies1965.png/330px-RhodesiaAllies1965.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RhodesiaAllies1965.png/440px-RhodesiaAllies1965.png 2x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="475"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:RhodesiaAllies1965.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The geopolitical situation during the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesian Bush War</a> in 1965 – countries friendly to the nationalists are coloured orange.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 3 September 1978, civilian airliner, <a href="/wiki/Air_Rhodesia_Flight_825" title="Air Rhodesia Flight 825">Air Rhodesia Flight 825</a>, was shot down near Kariba by the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_People%27s_Revolutionary_Army" title="Zimbabwe People's Revolutionary Army">Zimbabwe People's Revolutionary Army</a> (ZIPRA). 18 people, including children, survived the crash only for most of them to be shot by militants of the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_People%27s_Union" title="Zimbabwe African People's Union">Zimbabwe African People's Union</a> (ZAPU) led by <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Nkomo" title="Joshua Nkomo">Joshua Nkomo</a>. Rhodesia responded with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Gatling" title="Operation Gatling">Operation Gatling</a>, an attack on Nkomo's guerilla bases in Zambia, in particular, his military headquarters just outside Lusaka; this raid became known as the Green Leader Raid. On the same day, two more bases in Zambia were attacked using <a href="/wiki/Airpower" title="Airpower">air power</a> and elite <a href="/wiki/Paratrooper" title="Paratrooper">paratroops</a> and helicopter-borne troops.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/TAZARA_Railway" title="TAZARA Railway">railway</a> (TAZARA&nbsp;– Tanzania Zambia Railways) to the Tanzanian port of <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>, completed in 1975 with Chinese assistance, reduced Zambian dependence on railway lines south to South Africa and west through an increasingly troubled <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Angola" title="Portuguese Angola">Portuguese Angola</a>. Until the completion of the railway, Zambia's major artery for imports and the critical export of copper was along the TanZam Road, running from Zambia to the port cities in Tanzania. The <a href="/wiki/Tazama_Pipeline" title="Tazama Pipeline">Tazama oil pipeline</a> was also built from Dar es Salaam to <a href="/wiki/Ndola" title="Ndola">Ndola</a> in Zambia. </p>
      <p>By the late 1970s, Mozambique and Angola had attained independence from Portugal. Rhodesia's predominantly white government, which issued a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia's Unilateral Declaration of Independence">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> in 1965, accepted majority rule under the <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House Agreement</a> in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-zimstudy_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zimstudy-57">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Civil strife in both Portuguese colonies and a mounting <a href="/wiki/Namibian_War_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Namibian War of Independence">Namibian War of Independence</a> resulted in an influx of refugees<sup id="cite_ref-zastudy_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-58">[58]</a></sup> and compounded transportation issues. The <a href="/wiki/Benguela_railway" title="Benguela railway">Benguela railway</a>, which extended west through Angola, was essentially closed to Zambian traffic by the late 1970s. Zambia's support for <a href="/wiki/Internal_resistance_to_South_African_apartheid" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal resistance to South African apartheid">anti-apartheid</a> movements such as the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC) also created security problems as the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Defence_Force" title="South African Defence Force">South African Defence Force</a> struck at dissident targets during external raids.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_troubles">Economic troubles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economic troubles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In the mid-1970s, the price of copper, Zambia's principal export, suffered a severe decline worldwide. In Zambia's situation, the cost of transporting the copper great distances to the market was an additional strain. Zambia turned to foreign and international lenders for relief, but, as copper prices remained depressed, it became increasingly difficult to service its growing debt. By the mid-1990s, despite limited debt relief, Zambia's per capita foreign debt remained among the highest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratisation">Democratisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Democratisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In June 1990 riots against Kaunda accelerated. Many protesters were killed by the regime in breakthrough June 1990 protests.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 1990 Kaunda survived an <a href="/wiki/1990_Zambian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1990 Zambian coup d'état attempt">attempted coup</a>, and in 1991 he agreed to reinstate multiparty democracy, having instituted one-party rule under the Choma Commission of 1972. Following multiparty elections, Kaunda was removed from office (see below). </p>
      <p>In the 2000s, the economy stabilised, attaining single-digit inflation in 2006–2007, real GDP growth, decreasing interest rates, and increasing levels of trade. Much of its growth is due to foreign investment in mining and to higher world copper prices. All this led to Zambia being courted enthusiastically by aid donors and saw a surge in investor confidence in the country. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Zambia" title="Politics of Zambia">Politics of Zambia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg/220px-Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg/330px-Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg/440px-Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg 2x" data-file-width="670" data-file-height="447"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zambia_National_Assembly_Building.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Zambia National Assembly building in <a href="/wiki/Lusaka" title="Lusaka">Lusaka</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Politics in Zambia take place in a framework of a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic, whereby the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Zambia" title="President of Zambia">President of Zambia</a> is both <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a> in a pluriform <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party system</a>. The government exercises executive power, while legislative power is vested in both the government and parliament. </p>
      <p>Zambia became a republic immediately upon attaining independence in October 1964. From 2011 to 2014, Zambia's president had been Michael Sata, until Sata died on 28 October 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> After Sata's death, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Guy_Scott" title="Guy Scott">Guy Scott</a>, a Zambian of Scottish descent, became acting President of Zambia. Presidential elections were held on 22 January 2015. A total number of 11 presidential candidates contested in the election and On 24 January 2015, it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Lungu" title="Edgar Lungu">Edgar Chagwa Lungu</a> had won the <a href="/wiki/Zambian_presidential_election,_2015" class="mw-redirect" title="Zambian presidential election, 2015">election</a> to become the 6th President in a tightly contested race. He won 48.33% of the vote, a lead of 1.66% over his closest rival, <a href="/wiki/Hakainde_Hichilema" title="Hakainde Hichilema">Hakainde Hichilema</a>, with 46.67%.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> 9 other candidates all got less than 1% each. In August <a href="/wiki/2016_Zambian_general_election" title="2016 Zambian general election">2016 Zambian general election</a> president Edgar Lungu won re-election narrowly in the first round of the election. The opposition had allegations of fraud and the governing <a href="/wiki/Patriotic_Front_(Zambia)" title="Patriotic Front (Zambia)">Patriotic Front</a> (PF) rejected the allegations made by opposition UPND party.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-65">[65]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Zambia" title="Foreign relations of Zambia">Foreign relations of Zambia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu,_26_july_2018_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu%2C_26_july_2018_%282%29.jpg/220px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu%2C_26_july_2018_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu%2C_26_july_2018_%282%29.jpg/330px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu%2C_26_july_2018_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu%2C_26_july_2018_%282%29.jpg/440px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu%2C_26_july_2018_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1880" data-file-height="1160"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_and_Edgar_Lungu,_26_july_2018_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>President <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Lungu" title="Edgar Lungu">Edgar Lungu</a> with Russian President <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>, 26 July 2018
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After independence in 1964, the foreign relations of Zambia were mostly focused on supporting liberation movements in other countries in Southern Africa, such as the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> and <a href="/wiki/SWAPO" title="SWAPO">SWAPO</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, Zambia was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zambian_Defence_Force" title="Zambian Defence Force">Zambian Defence Force</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The Zambian Defence Force (ZDF) consists of the Zambia Army (ZA), the Zambia Air Force (ZAF), and the Zambian National Service (ZNS). The ZDF is designed primarily against external threats. </p>
      <p>In 2019, Zambia signed the UN <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Prohibition_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons">treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zambia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Zambia" title="Provinces of Zambia">Provinces of Zambia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="floatright">
       <div class="noresize" style="height: 311px; width: 360px; ">
        <map name="ImageMap_7ae7d3c95beaaa4d"><area href="/wiki/Western_Province,_Zambia" shape="rect" coords="19,197,91,261" alt="Western" title="Western"><area href="/wiki/North-Western_Province,_Zambia" shape="rect" coords="74,122,139,174" alt="North-Western" title="North-Western"><area href="/wiki/Copperbelt_Province" shape="rect" coords="163,136,201,168" alt="Copperbelt" title="Copperbelt"><area href="/wiki/Northern_Province,_Zambia" shape="rect" coords="238,17,296,72" alt="Northern" title="Northern"><area href="/wiki/Muchinga_Province" shape="rect" coords="286,87,329,116" alt="Muchinga" title="Muchinga"><area href="/wiki/Southern_Province,_Zambia" shape="rect" coords="118,237,176,277" alt="Southern" title="Southern"><area href="/wiki/Luapula_Province" shape="rect" coords="205,41,228,121" alt="Luapula" title="Luapula"><area href="/wiki/Central_Province,_Zambia" shape="rect" coords="151,178,242,202" alt="Central" title="Central"><area href="/wiki/Lusaka_Province" shape="rect" coords="208,208,254,233" alt="Lusaka" title="Lusaka"><area href="/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Zambia" shape="rect" coords="286,153,324,193" alt="Eastern" title="Eastern"></map>
